قَوْلُهُ ) وَالشَّرْعِيَّةُ مَا لَا يُدْرَكُ لَوْلَا خِطَابُ الشَّارِع ) بِنَفْسِ الْحُكْمِ أو بِأصْلِهِ الْمَقِيسِ هُوَ عَلَيْهِ فَيَخْرُجُ عَنْهَا مِثْلُ وُجُوبِ الْإِيمَانِ وَيَدْخُلُ مِثْلُ كَوْنِ الْإِجْمَاعِ أَوْ الْقِيَاسِ حُجَّةٌ عَلَى تَقْدِيرِ أَنْ يَكُونَ حُكْمًا ، وَإِنَّمَا لَمْ يُفَسِّرُ الشَّرْعِيَّةَ بِمَا وَرَدَ بهِ خِطَابُ الشَّرع ؛ لأنَّ التَّقْدِيرَ أَنَّ الْحُكْمَ مُفَسَّرٌ بِخِطَابِ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى إِلَى آخِرِهِ وَحِينَئِذٍ يَكُونُ تَقْسِيدُهُ بِالشَّرْعِ تَكْرَارًا وَعِندَ الْأَشَاعِرَةِ مَا وَرَدَ بِهِ خِطَابُ الشَّرْعِ فِي قُوَّةِ مَا لَا يُدْرَكُ لَوْلَا خِطَابُ الشَّرْعِ ، إِذْ لَا مَجَالَ لِلْعَقْلِ فِي دَرْكِ الْأَحْكَامِ فَلَوْ كَانَ خِطَابُ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى إِلَى آخِرِهِ تَعْرِيفًا لِلْحُكْمِ عَلَى مَا زَعَمَ الْمُصَنِّفُ لَا لِلْحُكْمِ الشَّرْعِيّ لَكَانَ ذِكْرُ الشَّرْعِي تَكْرَارًا أَلْبَيَّةَ بِأَيِّ تَفْسِيرٍ فُسِّرَ . قَوْلُهُ ) فَيَدْخُلُ ) يُرِيدُ أَنَّ تَعْرِيفَ الْفِقْهِ عَلَى رَأْيِ الْأَشَاعِرَةِ شَامِلٌ لِلْعِلْمِ عَنْ دَلِيلٍ بِحُسْنِ الْجُودِ وَالتَّوَاضُع أى وُجُوبِهِمَا أَوْ نَدْبِهِمَا وَقُبح الْبُخْلِ وَالتَّكبر أى جُرَمَتِهِمَا أَوْ كَرَاهَتِهِمَا وَمَا أَشْبَهَ ذَلِكَ ؛ لأنَّهَا أَحْكَامٌ لَا تُدْرَكُ لَوْلَا خِطَابُ الشَّرْعِ عَلَى رَأْيِهِمْ بِنَاءً عَلَى أَنْ لَّا مَدْخَلَ لِلْعَقْلِ فِي دَرِّكِ الْأَحْكَامِ مَعَ أَنَّ الْعِلْمَ بِهَا مِنْ عِلْمِ الْأَخْلاقِ لَا مِنْ عِلْمِ الْفِقْهِ وَأقُولُ إِنَّمَا يَلْزَمُ ذَلِكَ لَوْ كَانَتْ هَذِهِ الأحْكَامُ عَمَلِيَّةٌ بِالْمَعْنَى الْمَذْكُورِ ، وَهُوَ مَمْنُوعٌ كَيْفَ وَالْأُمُورُ الْمَذْكُورَةُ أَخلاق مَلَكَاتٌ نَفْسَانِيَّةٌ جَعَلَ الْمُصَنِّفُ الْعِلْمَ بِحُسُنِهَا وَقُبْحِهَا مِنْ عِلْمِ الْأَخْلَاقِ وَقَدْ صَرَّحَ فِيمَا سَبَقَ بِأَنَّهُ يُزَادُ عَمَلًا عَلَى مَعْرِفَةِ النَّفْسِ مَا لَهَا وَمَا عَلَيْهَا لِيَخْرُجَ عِلمُ الْأَخْلَاقِ وَبِأَنَّ مَعْرِفَةَ مَا لَهَا وَمَا عَلَيْهَا مِنَ الْوِجُدَانِيَّاتِ أَى الْأَخْلَاقِ الْبَاطِنِيَّةِ وَالْمَلَكَاتِ النَّفْسَانِيَّةِ عِلْمُ الْأَخْلَاقِ ، وَمِنْ الْعَمَلِيَّاتِ عِلْمُ الْفِقْهِ فَكَأَنَّهُ نَسِيَ مَا ذَكَرَهُ ثَمَّه أَوْ ذَهَلَ عَنْ قَيْدِ الْعَمَلِيَّةِ ههنا .ترجمہ:۔ اس کا قول و الشرعیہ الخ یعنی شرعیہ وہ ہے کہ نہ ادراک کیا جائے اگر نہ ہو شارع کا خطاب پھر شارع کا خطاب یا نفس حکم میں ہوگا یا ایسی اصل پر ہوگا کہ جس پر کسی دوسرے حکم کو قیاس کیا جائے پس نکل جائیں گے اس سے وجوب ایمان کی مثل اور داخل ہو جائے گا اجماع اور قیاس کے حجت ہونے کی مثل اس تقدیر پر کہ وہ حکم ہو اور سوائے اس کے نہیں تفسیر کی الشرعیہ کی ماورد بہ خطاب الشارع کیساتھ اسلئے فرض یہ ہے کہ حکم کی تفسیر کی گئی ہے خطاب اللہ الخ کے ساتھ اور اس وقت ہوگا اسکو مقید
His statement: 'And al-shar'iyya (the shar'i ruling) is that which cannot be perceived without the address of the Lawgiver' — either by the ruling itself or by its principle upon which it is analogized. Thus, similar to the obligation of faith is excluded, and similar to the consensus or analogy being proof (hujja) is included, on the assumption that it is a ruling. And he did not define al-shar'iyya as 'that which the address of the Lawgiver has come with' because the assumption is that the ruling is defined by 'the address of Allah, etc.' and then restricting it by 'shar'i' would be repetitive. And according to the Ash'aris, 'what the address of the Lawgiver has come with' is like 'that which cannot be perceived without the address of the Lawgiver,' since there is no room for reason in perceiving rulings. So if 'the address of Allah, etc.' were a definition of ruling (not specifically shar'i ruling) as the author claimed, then mentioning 'shar'i' would be definitely repetitive, regardless of how it is interpreted. His statement: 'Thus includes' — he intends that the definition of fiqh according to the Ash'ari view includes knowledge by evidence of the goodness of generosity and humility (meaning their obligation or recommendation) and the ugliness of stinginess and arrogance (meaning their prohibition or dislike), and similar things, because they are rulings that cannot be perceived without the address of the Lawgiver according to them, based on the view that reason has no entry in perceiving rulings. However, knowledge of these belongs to ethics (ilm al-akhlaq), not fiqh. And I say: that would only follow if these rulings were 'amaliyya (practical) in the mentioned sense, and that is denied. How so? The mentioned matters are ethical, psychological traits; the author made knowledge of their goodness and ugliness part of ethics, and he explicitly stated earlier that 'amal (practice) is added to self-knowledge (what it has and what it owes) to exclude ethics, and that knowledge of what it has and what it owes from the intuitive matters (i.e., internal ethics and psychological traits) is ethics, and from the practical matters is fiqh. It is as if he forgot what he mentioned there or was heedless of the restriction of 'amaliyya here. Translation: His saying 'wa al-shar'iyya etc.' means: shar'iyya is that which cannot be perceived if there is no address of the Lawgiver. Then the address of the Lawgiver either pertains to the ruling itself or to a principle upon which another ruling is analogized. So similar to the obligation of faith is excluded, and similar to consensus or analogy being a proof is included, on the assumption that it is a ruling. And he did not explain al-shar'iyya by 'what the address of the Lawgiver has come with' because the assumption is that the ruling is explained by 'the address of Allah, etc.' and then it would be restricted [repetitively].
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage explains the technical definition of al-hukm al-shar'i (the shar'i ruling) as that which cannot be known without divine revelation. It is part of a commentary on Al-Tawdih (the matn). The author first clarifies that the definition excludes purely rational obligations like belief in God (wujub al-iman), and includes the evidential value of ijma' and qiyas if considered rulings. He then explains why the definition was not given as 'what the Lawgiver's address has come with' because that would be redundant given the definition of 'ruling' already refers to divine address. Then he contrasts the Ash'ari position: they consider that all rulings are known only through revelation, so for them, the definition of fiqh would include knowledge of ethical virtues (like generosity being good) which are also known only through revelation. However, the author (Hanafi) argues that such knowledge belongs to 'ilm al-akhlaq (ethics), not fiqh, because fiqh deals with practical rulings ('amaliyya), not inner traits. He criticizes the author of the matn for seeming to forget that he himself previously distinguished between ethics and fiqh by adding 'amal (practice) to define fiqh as practical knowledge. This passage carefully delineates the scope of fiqh and the definition of shar'i rulings, emphasizing the Hanafi view that reason can know some things (like the obligation of faith) without revelation, but fiqh deals only with rulings derived from divine address.

Key Terms
الشرعية (al-shar'iyya): A ruling that cannot be known without divine address (khitab al-shari'), either directly or by analogy.
خطاب الشارع (khitab al-shari'): The address of the Lawgiver (Allah or His Messenger) that conveys a ruling.
العملية (al-'amaliyya): Practical rulings related to external actions of the mukallaf, as opposed to inner ethical states.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view presented in this passage, why is 'obligation of faith' (wujub al-iman) excluded from the definition of al-hukm al-shar'i?
A) Because it is not a ruling at all.
B) Because it can be known by reason without divine address.
C) Because it is not a practical ('amali) ruling.
D) Because it is not mentioned in the Quran or Sunnah.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of shar'i ruling is restricted to practical matters known only through revelation, excluding both rational duties and inner ethical traits, which belong to ethics.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
10 min
The Shafi'i Condition for Fiqh: Acquisition Excluding Necessary Knowledge
Analysis of the Shafi'i condition in the definition of fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Shafi'i school requires that fiqh be acquired through nazar and istidlal, and identify the practical consequence of excluding necessarily known rulings.

Core Idea
Fiqh is acquired knowledge; necessary knowledge is not fiqh technically.

The definition of fiqh is central to usul al-fiqh. This passage explores a key dispute: should fiqh include matters known by necessity (like the obligation of prayer)? The Shafi'i school explicitly excludes them, while the Hanafi definition focuses on practical rulings without that explicit condition. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind the Shafi'i condition and how it shapes the scope of fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Shafi'i condition for fiqh
Locate in the passage the statement that fiqh is only called so if obtained through nazar (reflection) and istidlal (deduction). Note the example: knowledge of wajib al-salat is not fiqh in terminology.
The condition excludes daruri (necessary) knowledge.
2
Understand the rationale behind the condition
Why is necessary knowledge excluded? Because it does not require ijtihad; anyone, regardless of religious commitment, knows these basics. Fiqh implies effort and derivation.
This is a qualifier of iktisab (acquisition).
3
Apply the condition to the Hanafi definition
The Hanafi definition of fiqh as 'knowledge of practical shar'i rulings from detailed evidence' does not explicitly exclude necessary knowledge. However, the passage shows that Hanafis still consider fiqh as derived knowledge. Consider the practical difference: a person who knows only the obligation of prayer without proof is a faqih? According to Shafi'is, no.
The difference is in technical terminology.
4
Analyze the objection about inner ethics
The passage raises an objection: does the definition wrongly include inner ethics (akhlaq batina) like arrogance? The response clarifies that the effects of these traits are actions (e.g., not giving zakah), so they are covered by 'amaliyyah (practical). Thus, the definition excludes them only if they are not action-related.
This shows the need for precise wording in definitions.

جواب:۔ دیا کہ ماورد به خطاب الشرع سے تعریف اس لئے نہیں کی کہ اس کا معنی ہے جو ماخوذ من الشرع ہو اور ماتن کے نزدیک فقہ کی تعریف میں حکم سے مراد حکم اصطلاحی ہے (خطاب اللہ الخ ) اس کا معنی بھی ماخوذ من الشریعہ ہے اب اگر الشریعہ کا معنیٰ ماخوذ من الشرع کریں تو تکرار لازم آئے گا تو تکرار سے بچنے کے لئے ما توقف علی الشریعۃ سے تعریف کر دی اب تکرار لازم نہیں آئے گا کیونکہ اس صورت میں وجوب ایمان خارج ہو گا یہ شریعت پر موقوف نہیں ہے۔ شاریخ کا ماتن پر اعتراض
وعند الاشاعرہ :۔ یہ شرح کی عبارت کا تیسرا حصہ ہے غرض شارح ماتن پر اعتراض کرنا ہے کہ یہ تعریف فقہ اشاعرہ سے منقول ہے اور آپ خود بنا چکے ہیں کہ اشاعرہ کے نزدیک ہر چیز کا حسن و فتح شریعت پر موقوف ہے عقل کو دخل نہیں ہے حسن و قبح میں جب ہر چیز کا حسن و صبح شریعت پر موقوف ہے تو اشاعرہ کے نزدیک شریعت کا معنی ما توقف علی الشریعہ سے کریں یا ماورد به خطاب الشریعہ سے کریں دونوں کا مفہوم ایک ہے اب بقول مصنف کے اگر فقہ کی تعریف میں حکم سے مراد خطاب الله .. الخ ہو تو آگے الشریعہ کا جو معنی کریں بہر صورت تکرار ہوگا کیونکہ ماورد به خطاب الشریعہ یہ ان کے نزدیک ما توقف علی الشریعہ کے معنی میں ہے لہذا الشریعہ کا معنی اگر ماورد به خطاب الشرع سے کریں تو تکرار لازم آئے گا تو مصنف کا یہ کہنا کہ الشریعہ کا معنیٰ اگر ماورد به خطاب الشرع سے کریں تو تکرار لازم آئے گا اور اگر ما توقف علی الشرع سے کریں تو پھر تکرار لازم نہیں آئے گا یہ درست نہیں ہے۔
جواب: - تکرار تب لازم آئے گا جب یہ قید کسی فائدہ پر مشتمل نہ ہو ہم کہتے ہیں کہ یہ تصریح بما علم التزاما کے قبیل سے ہے علامہ تفتازانی پر حیرت ہے جب مصنف نے امام رازی پر اعتراض کیا تھا کہ فقہ کی تعریف میں استدلال کی قید مکرر ہے تو شاریخ نے جواب دیا تھا کہ یہ تصریح بما علم ضمنا کے قبیل سے ہے تو یہاں بھی یہ کہا جا سکتا ہے۔ باقی اگر کوئی یہ اعتراض کرے کہ ماتن نے تو وہاں تصریح بما علم التزاما کا اعتبار نہیں کیا تو یہاں اس کا اعتبار نہیں کرنا چاہیے تھا تو اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ ماتن اس کا اعتبار کرتے ہیں لیکن وہاں استدلال کی قید تصریح بما علم التزاما کے قبیل میں سے نہیں ہے اس کی تفصیل گزرچکی ہے اشاعرہ کرام کی یہ تعریف دخول غیر سے مانع نہیں
قولہ فید نخل :۔ یہ شرح کی عبارت کا پہلا حصہ ہے اس عبارت میں متن کی وضاحت ہے ماتن نے اعتراض کیا تھا اسکی تفصیل کرنا مقصود ہے کہ فقہ کی تعریف دخول غیر سے مانع نہیں ہے کیونکہ فقہ کی تعریف اخلاق باطنہ بجل وغیرہ پر صادق آئے گی
حالانکہ یہ علم فقہ نہیں اور یہ ایسے صادق آئے گی کہ اشاعرہ کے نزدیک ہر شئی کا حسن و قبح شریعت پر موقوف ہے لہذا اخلاق باطنہ کا حسن و قیح بھی شریعت پر موقوف ہوگا تو یہ بھی احکام شرعی میں داخل ہو نگے ۔
اقول: یہ شرح کی عبارت کا دوسرا حصہ ہے غرض اس سے ماتن پر اعتراض کرتا ہے۔
اعتراض: اخلاق باطنہ مثلا زہد، تکبر و غیرہ اگر چہ حکم شرعی میں داخل ہونگے لیکن العملیہ کی قید سے فقہ سے خارج ہو جائینگے کیونکہ ان کا تعلق دل سے ہے عمل سے نہیں اس پر قرینہ یہ ہے کہ مالہا و ما علیہا میں کہا تھا کہ اگر اخلاق باطنہ کو فقہ سے خارج کرنا ہو تو عملا کی قید لگا ئیں گے تو اس تعریف میں جو احناف نے کی ہے اخلاق باطنہ عملا کی قید سے نکل جاتے ہیں ہماری تعریف سے کیوں نہیں نکلتے ایسے لگتا ہے کہ ماتن کو وہ بات بھول گئی ہے یا عملیہ کی قید نہیں دیکھی۔
جواب:۔ دیا کہ مائن نے جو کہا جو دوڑہد کا حسن، بخل و تکبر کا فتح یہ حکم شرعی میں داخل ہیں تو اس سے مراد نفس جو دو ہر نہیں لیا بلکہ ان کے آثار مراد لئے ہیں یعنی بخل کا اثر یہ ہے کہ صدقات واجبہ نہیں دے گا یہ عمل ہے سخاوت کا اثر یہ ہے کہ نفلی صدقات دے گا تکبر کا اثر یہ ہے کہ اللہ تعالیٰ کی اطاعت نہیں کرے گا عاجزی کا اثر یہ ہے کہ اللہ تعالیٰ کے ساتھ ان کی بھی اطاعت کرے گا جن کی اطاعت کا اللہ تعالیٰ نے حکم دیا ہے اب جب اخلاق باطنہ سے مراد ان کے آثار ہیں تو عملا کی قید سے خارج نہیں ہونگے تو تعریف فقہ دخول غیر سے مانع نہ ہوگی۔
قَوْلُهُ ) وَلَا يُزَادُ عَلَيْهِ الْمُصْطَلَحُ بَيْنَ الشَّافِعِيَّةِ أَنَّ الْعِلْمَ بِالْأَحْكَامِ إِنَّمَا يُسَمَّى فِقْهَا إِذَا كَانَ حُصُولُهُ بِطَرِيقِ النَّظَرِ وَالاسْتِدْلالِ حَتَّى أَنَّ الْعِلْمَ بِوُجُوبِ الصَّلاةِ وَالصَّوْمِ وَنَحْوِ ذَلِكَ مِمَّا اشْتَهَرَ كَوْنُهُ مِنْ الدِّينِ بِالضَّرُورَةِ بِحَيْثُ يَعْلَمُهُ الْمُتَدَيْنُ وَغَيْرُهُ لَا يُعَدُّ مِنْ الْفِقْهِ اصْطِلَاحًا وَلِهَذَا يَذْكُرُونَ قَيْدَ الاكْتِسَابِ. وَالاسْتِدْلالِ فَالْإِمَامُ قَيَّدَ فِي الْمَحْصُولِ الأحْكَامَ بِالَّتِي لَا يُعْلَمُ كَوْنُهَا مِنَ الدِّينِ بِالضَّرُورَةِ، وَقَالَ هُوَ احْتِرَازٌ عَنْ الْعِلْمِ بِوُجُوبِ الصَّلَاةِ وَالصَّوْمِ فَإِنَّهُ لَا يُسَمَّى فِقُهَا بِمَعْنَى أَنَّهُ لَا يَدْخُلُ فِى مُسَمَّى الْفِقْهِ وَلَا يُعَدُّ مِنْهُ عَلَى مَا صَرَّحَ بِهِ فِي قَيْدِ الْعَمَيَّةِ لَا بِمَعْنَى أَنَّهُ لَرُ لَمْ يُحْتَرَزُ عَنْهُ لَزِمَ أَنْ يَكُونَ الْعَالِمُ بِمُجَرَّدِ وُجُوبِهِمَا فَقِيها عَلَى مَا فَهِمَهُ الْمُصَنِّفُ فَاعْتَرَض : مَنْعِ لَوم ذَلكَ بِنَاء عَلَى أَنَّ الْفَقِيهَ مَنْ لَهُ الْفِقْهُ
Answer: It was said that the definition with 'what is brought by the address of the Sharia' is not made because its meaning is that which is taken from the Sharia, and according to the author, in the definition of fiqh, 'hukm' means the technical ruling (address of Allah, etc.) and its meaning is also taken from the Sharia. Now if the meaning of 'Sharia' is taken as 'taken from the Sharia', then repetition would be necessary. So to avoid repetition, it was defined with 'what depends on the Sharia'. Now repetition is not necessary because in that case, the obligation of faith is excluded; it does not depend on the Sharia. The commentator's objection to the author. According to the Ash'aris: This is the third part of the commentary's statement. The commentator intends to object to the author that this definition of fiqh is transmitted from the Ash'aris, and you yourself have established that according to the Ash'aris, the goodness and evil of everything depends on the Sharia; reason has no entry in goodness and evil. When the goodness and evil of everything depends on the Sharia, then according to the Ash'aris, whether the meaning of 'Sharia' is taken as 'what depends on the Sharia' or 'what is brought by the address of the Sharia', their meaning is the same. Now according to the author, if in the definition of fiqh, 'hukm' means the address of Allah, etc., then whatever meaning you give later for 'Sharia', repetition will occur in any case, because 'what is brought by the address of the Sharia' is, in their view, in the meaning of 'what depends on the Sharia'. Therefore, if the meaning of 'Sharia' is taken as 'what is brought by the address of the Sharia', repetition would be necessary. So the author's claim that if the meaning of 'Sharia' is taken as 'what is brought by the address of the Sharia', then repetition would be necessary, and if taken as 'what depends on the Sharia', then repetition is not necessary – this is not correct. Answer: Repetition is only necessary when this qualifier does not contain any benefit. We say that this is of the category of clarification of what is known by commitment. It is surprising for Allama Taftazani: when the author objected to Imam Razi that in the definition of fiqh, the qualifier of istidlal is repeated, the commentator answered that this is of the category of clarification of what is known implicitly, then here too it can be said. Otherwise, if someone objects that the author did not consider clarification of what is known by commitment there, then here it should not be considered. The answer is that the author does consider it, but there the qualifier of istidlal is not of the category of clarification of what is known by commitment; its detail has passed. The Ash'ari definition does not prevent the inclusion of others. His statement: 'fa yadkhul' [؟] – This is the first part of the commentary's statement. In this statement, there is an explanation of the text. The author had objected; its detail is intended: that the definition of fiqh does not prevent the inclusion of others, because the definition of fiqh would apply to inner ethics, etc., although this is not the science of fiqh. And it would apply in such a way that, according to the Ash'aris, the goodness and evil of everything depends on the Sharia; therefore, the goodness and evil of inner ethics also depend on the Sharia, so they too would be included in the rulings of the Sharia. I say: This is the second part of the commentary's statement; with it, the commentator objects to the author. Objection: Inner ethics like asceticism, arrogance, etc., although they would be included in the rulings of the Sharia, but the qualifier 'amaliyyah' would exclude them from fiqh because they relate to the heart, not action. The indication is that in 'mā lahā wa mā 'alayhā' it was said that if inner ethics are to be excluded from fiqh, then the qualifier 'amali' would be added. So in the definition that the Hanafis made, inner ethics are excluded by the qualifier 'amali'. Why are they not excluded from our definition? It seems that the author either forgot that or did not see the qualifier 'amaliyyah'. Answer: It was said that what the author said that the goodness of [؟], the evil of miserliness and arrogance – these are included in the rulings of the Sharia – but by this, he did not mean the essence of miserliness itself, but rather their effects. That is, the effect of miserliness is that he will not give obligatory charity – this is an action. The effect of generosity is that he will give voluntary charity. The effect of arrogance is that he will not obey Allah. The effect of humility is that he will obey Allah and also obey those whose obedience Allah has commanded. Now when by inner ethics their effects are meant, then they will not be excluded by the qualifier 'amali', so the definition of fiqh will not prevent the inclusion of others. His statement: 'And it is not added to it: the term according to the Shafi'is is that knowledge of rulings is only called fiqh if it is obtained through the method of reflection and deduction, such that knowledge of the obligation of prayer and fasting and similar matters that are well-known to be of the religion by necessity, to the extent that the religious person and others know it, is not counted as fiqh in terminology. And for this reason, they mention the qualifiers of acquisition and deduction. So Imam al-Razi in al-Mahsul restricted rulings to those whose being from the religion is not known by necessity. He said: this is a precaution against knowledge of the obligation of prayer and fasting; it is not called fiqh, meaning it does not enter into the name of fiqh and is not counted from it, as he clarified in the qualifier of 'amaliyyah [؟] – not in the sense that if it were not guarded against, then the one who knows merely their obligation would be a faqih, as the author understood and objected: preventing blame for that, based on the fact that the faqih is one who has fiqh.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage is an extended commentary on the definition of fiqh, addressing several objections and clarifications. It begins by answering why the definition uses 'what depends on the Sharia' rather than 'what is brought by the address of the Sharia' – to avoid repetition (takrar) because both phrases would essentially mean the same thing if not carefully distinguished. The commentator then objects to the author (Matan) on the Ash'ari view, arguing that according to Ash'aris, everything's goodness and evil is determined by Sharia, so the two phrases are synonymous, making repetition inevitable. The response invokes the principle of 'clarification of what is known by commitment' (tasrih bima 'ulima iltizaman), citing Taftazani and earlier discussions on Imam Razi's definition. Next, the passage addresses the objection that the definition does not exclude inner ethics (akhlaq batina) like asceticism and arrogance. The commentator argues that these are not intrinsic actions but their effects (e.g., miserliness leads to not giving obligatory charity), which are actions ('amali), so the qualifier 'amaliyyah excludes them. Finally, the Shafi'i definition is presented: fiqh is knowledge obtained through ijtihad and istidlal, excluding necessarily known matters like the obligation of prayer. Imam Razi in al-Mahsul added this qualifier, and the author's objection is rejected by clarifying that 'faqih' is not merely one who knows a few obligatory acts but one who has comprehensive knowledge of fiqh through deduction.

Key Terms
الفقه (al-fiqh): Knowledge of the practical shar'i rulings derived from detailed evidence
الاستدلال (al-istidlal): Deduction from evidence, including qiyas and other methods
الاكسساب (al-iktisab): Acquisition (of knowledge through effort and reasoning)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Shafi'i definition of fiqh as presented in this passage, why is knowledge of wajib al-salat (obligation of prayer) not considered fiqh?
A) Because prayer is not a practical ruling
B) Because such knowledge is known by necessity and not obtained through ijtihad and istidlal
C) Because it is not derived from the Quran and Sunnah
D) Because it is not part of the science of fiqh

Key Takeaway
The Shafi'i definition restricts fiqh to knowledge acquired through ijtihad and istidlal, excluding matters known by necessity—a terminological precision that does not change the essential content of fiqh.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
10 min
The Definition of Fiqh: Shafi'i Condition of Istidlal vs. Hanafi Position
Analyzing the Shafi'i condition that fiqh must be acquired through nazar and istidlal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Shafi'i condition for fiqh and contrast it with the Hanafi definition, identifying the practical consequence of the terminological difference.

Core Idea
Fiqh requires derivation from detailed evidence; necessary knowledge is excluded by both schools, but Shafi'is make it explicit while Hanafis consider it inherent.

This passage from a sharh of Al-Talwih analyzes a key terminological difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding what counts as fiqh. The Shafi'is add an explicit condition that fiqh must be acquired through nazar (reflection) and istidlal (deduction), thereby excluding necessary knowledge (daruriyyat) like the obligation of prayer. The Hanafi commentator defends their own definition, arguing that the exclusion is already implicit and that each school's terminology is valid. Understanding this dispute clarifies how the two schools view the scope of fiqh as a discipline.

Guided Path
1
State the Shafi'i terminological condition
Identify the Shafi'i requirement that knowledge of rulings is called fiqh only if obtained through nazar (reflection) and istidlal (deduction). Note that this excludes daruriyyat (necessary knowledge) like the obligation of prayer and fasting.
The Shafi'i position is explicitly about the method of acquisition, not just the source.
2
Identify the Hanafi definition of fiqh
Recall the Hanafi definition: knowledge of practical Sharia rulings derived from detailed evidence (adilla tafsiliyya). This definition implicitly excludes necessary knowledge because such knowledge is not derived from detailed evidence.
The Hanafi definition does not require an explicit condition of nazar/istidlal.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i condition
The passage argues that the Hanafi definition already excludes daruriyyat without an extra condition. Furthermore, it invokes the principle 'la mushahata fi al-istilah' (no dispute over terminology), accepting the Shafi'i addition as valid for their own school.
This shows a meta-legal tolerance: both definitions are legitimate within their respective terminological frameworks.
4
Examine the objection regarding inner morals (akhlaq batina)
An earlier objection claimed that inner morals like arrogance or humility might enter the definition of fiqh. The commentator answers that such terms refer to their external effects (athar), which are actions, thus falling under the restriction of 'amaliyya (practical rulings).
This clarifies that the Hanafi definition's 'amaliyya condition effectively excludes non-action-related inner states.
5
Extract the thamarah al-khilaf and the terminological principle
The practical consequence (thamarah) is that what is considered fiqh differs: a ruling known by necessity is fiqh for a Hanafi but not for a Shafi'i terminologically. The resolution is that both definitions are valid, and no objection can be raised against them due to the principle of la mushahata fi al-istilah.
The dispute is terminological, not substantive; it does not affect actual legal rulings.

Source Text

حالانکہ یہ علم فقہ نہیں اور یہ ایسے صادق آئے گی کہ اشاعرہ کے نزدیک ہر شئی کا حسن و قبح شریعت پر موقوف ہے لہذا اخلاق باطنہ کا حسن و قیح بھی شریعت پر موقوف ہوگا تو یہ بھی احکام شرعی میں داخل ہو نگے ۔
اقول: یہ شرح کی عبارت کا دوسرا حصہ ہے غرض اس سے ماتن پر اعتراض کرتا ہے۔
اعتراض: اخلاق باطنہ مثلا زہد، تکبر و غیرہ اگر چہ حکم شرعی میں داخل ہونگے لیکن العملیہ کی قید سے فقہ سے خارج ہو جائینگے کیونکہ ان کا تعلق دل سے ہے عمل سے نہیں اس پر قرینہ یہ ہے کہ مالہا و ما علیہا میں کہا تھا کہ اگر اخلاق باطنہ کو فقہ سے خارج کرنا ہو تو عملا کی قید لگا ئیں گے تو اس تعریف میں جو احناف نے کی ہے اخلاق باطنہ عملا کی قید سے نکل جاتے ہیں ہماری تعریف سے کیوں نہیں نکلتے ایسے لگتا ہے کہ ماتن کو وہ بات بھول گئی ہے یا عملیہ کی قید نہیں دیکھی۔
جواب:۔ دیا کہ مائن نے جو کہا جو دوڑہد کا حسن، بخل و تکبر کا فتح یہ حکم شرعی میں داخل ہیں تو اس سے مراد نفس جو دو ہر نہیں لیا بلکہ ان کے آثار مراد لئے ہیں یعنی بخل کا اثر یہ ہے کہ صدقات واجبہ نہیں دے گا یہ عمل ہے سخاوت کا اثر یہ ہے کہ نفلی صدقات دے گا تکبر کا اثر یہ ہے کہ اللہ تعالیٰ کی اطاعت نہیں کرے گا عاجزی کا اثر یہ ہے کہ اللہ تعالیٰ کے ساتھ ان کی بھی اطاعت کرے گا جن کی اطاعت کا اللہ تعالیٰ نے حکم دیا ہے اب جب اخلاق باطنہ سے مراد ان کے آثار ہیں تو عملا کی قید سے خارج نہیں ہونگے تو تعریف فقہ دخول غیر سے مانع نہ ہوگی۔
قَوْلُهُ ) وَلَا يُزَادُ عَلَيْهِ الْمُصْطَلَحُ بَيْنَ الشَّافِعِيَّةِ أَنَّ الْعِلْمَ بِالْأَحْكَامِ إِنَّمَا يُسَمَّى فِقْهَا إِذَا كَانَ حُصُولُهُ بِطَرِيقِ النَّظَرِ وَالاسْتِدْلالِ حَتَّى أَنَّ الْعِلْمَ بِوُجُوبِ الصَّلاةِ وَالصَّوْمِ وَنَحْوِ ذَلِكَ مِمَّا اشْتَهَرَ كَوْنُهُ مِنْ الدِّينِ بِالضَّرُورَةِ بِحَيْثُ يَعْلَمُهُ الْمُتَدَيْنُ وَغَيْرُهُ لَا يُعَدُّ مِنْ الْفِقْهِ اصْطِلَاحًا وَلِهَذَا يَذْكُرُونَ قَيْدَ الاكْتِسَابِ. وَالاسْتِدْلالِ فَالْإِمَامُ قَيَّدَ فِي الْمَحْصُولِ الأحْكَامَ بِالَّتِي لَا يُعْلَمُ كَوْنُهَا مِنَ الدِّينِ بِالضَّرُورَةِ، وَقَالَ هُوَ احْتِرَازٌ عَنْ الْعِلْمِ بِوُجُوبِ الصَّلَاةِ وَالصَّوْمِ فَإِنَّهُ لَا يُسَمَّى فِقُهَا بِمَعْنَى أَنَّهُ لَا يَدْخُلُ فِى مُسَمَّى الْفِقْهِ وَلَا يُعَدُّ مِنْهُ عَلَى مَا صَرَّحَ بِهِ فِي قَيْدِ الْعَمَلِيَّةِ لَا بِمَعْنَى أَنَّهُ لَمْ يُحْتَرَزُ عَنْهُ لَزِمَ أَنْ يَكُونَ الْعَالِمُ بِمُجَرَّدِ وُجُوبِهِمَا فَقِيها عَلَى مَا فَهِمَهُ الْمُصَنِّفُ فَاعْتَرَض : مَنْعِ لَوم ذَلكَ بِنَاء عَلَى أَنَّ الْفَقِيهَ مَنْ لَهُ الْفِقْهُ وَالْفِقْهُ لَيْسَ عِلْمًا بِبَعْضِ الْأَحْكَامِ ، وَإِنْ قَلَّ حَتَّى يَكُونَ الْعَالِمُ بِمَسْأَلَةٍ أَوْ مَسْأَلَتَيْنِ فَقِيها ، بَلْ الْعَالِمُ بِمِائَةٍ مَسْأَلَةٍ غَرِيبَةٍ اسْتِدْلَالِيَّةٍ وَحُدَهَا لَا يُسَمَّى فَقِيهَا ، ثُمَّ إِذَا كَانَ اصْطِلاحُهُمْ عَلَى أَنَّ الْعِلْمَ بِضَرُورِيَّاتِ الدِّينِ لَيْسَ مِنْ الْفِقْهِ فَلَا بُدَّ مِنْ إخْرَاجِهَا عَنْ تَعْرِيفِهِمُ الْفِقْهَ فَلا يَكُونُ الْقَيْدُ الْمُخْرِجُ لَهَا صَائِعًا وَلَا الْقَوْلُ بِكَوْنِهَا مِنَ الْفِقْهِ صَحِيحًا عِندَهُمْ وَلَا الاصْطِلاحُ عَلَى ذَلِكَ صَالِحًا لِلاعْتِرَاضِ عَلَيْهِمُ . ترجمہ: شوافع کے درمیان مصطلح ہے کہ بیشک علم بالا حکام سوائے اس کے نام رکھا جاتا ہے اس کا فقہ کہ جب اس کا حاصل کرنا بطریق نظر اور بطریق استدلال ہو یہاں تک کہ بیشک علم بوجوب الصلوۃ اور لوجوب الصوم اور اس کی مثل جو مشہور ہو گیا ہے ہونا اس کے دین سے بدلہہ اس حیثیت سے کہ جانتے ہیں اس کو دیندار اور اس کے علاوہ بھی نہیں شمار کیا جا تافقہ سے اصطلاحاً اس وجہ سے ذکر کرتے ہیں وہ اکتاب اور استدلال کی قید کو اور امام صاحب نے مقید کیا اسکو محصول میں اللتی لا علم کو نہ من الدين بالضرورۃ کیسا تھ اور کہا کہ وہ احتراز ہے علم بوجوب الصلوۃ سے اور بوجوب الصوم سے پس بیشک وہ نہیں نام رکھا جا تا فقہ اس معنی میں کہ وہ داخل نہ ہو فقہ کے نام میں اور نہیں شمار کیا جائے گا اس سے جیسا کہ تصریح کی ہے امام نے العملیہ کی قید میں نہ کہ اس معنی کے ساتھ کہ بیشک اگر نہ احتراز کرتا اس سے تو لازم آتا یہ کہ ہو عالم فقط ان دونوں کے وجوب کا فقیہ۔ اوپر اس کے جس کو مصنف نے سمجھا پس اعتراض کیا اس کے لزوم کے منع کے ساتھ بناء کرتے ہوئے اس بات پر کہ بیٹک فقیہ وہ شخص ہے کہ جس کو فقہ حاصل ہو اور فقہ نہیں ہے جاننا بعض احکام کا اگر چہ کم ہو یہاں تک کہ ہو جاننے والا ایک مسئلہ کو یا دو مسئلوں کو فقیہ بلکہ جاننے والا ۱۰۰ مسائل غریبہ استدلالیہ کو بھی علیحدہ طور پر نہیں نام رکھا جاتا فقیہ ۔ پھر جب ہے ان کی اصطلاح اس بات پر کہ بیشک کہ ضروریات دین کا جاننا نہیں ہے فقہ سے پھر ضروری تھا نکالنا اس کو تعریف فقہ سے پس نہیں ہوگی وہ قید جونکالنے والی ہے ضائع اور نہ ہو گا قول کرنا اس کے فقہ سے ہونے کے ساتھ ان کے نزدیک صحیح اور نہیں ہے اس پر ایسی اصطلاح قائم کرنا جو صلاحیت رکھنے والی ہو اعتراض کرنے کے لئے ان پر ۔
تشریح : قولہ ولا یز اود علیہ :۔ اس قول میں شاریخ نے ماتن پر اعتراض کیا ہے جس کی تفصیل یہ ہے کہ بعض احکام نظری اور استدلالی ہیں یعنی دلیل پر موقوف ہیں اور بعض احکام بدیہی ہیں کہ جن کے لئے استدلال و نظر و فکر کی ضرورت نہیں اب شوافع نے اصطلاح قائم کی ہے کہ ہمارے نزدیک فقہ احکام نظریہ کا نام ہے اور احکام بدیہہ کے جانے کا نام فقہ نہیں اور ہر گروہ کو اصطلاح قائم کرنے کا حق حاصل ہے کیونکہ ضابطہ ہے لا مشاحة في الاصطلاح اب جب انہوں نے اصطلاح قائم کرلی
While this is not fiqh, and it will come as such that according to the Ash'aris, the beauty and ugliness of everything depends on the Sharia, so the beauty and ugliness of inner morals will also depend on the Sharia, so these will also enter into the Sharia rulings. I say: This is the second part of the sharh's statement; in short, it objects to the matn. Objection: Inner morals like zuhd, arrogance, etc., although they enter into Sharia rulings, they will be excluded from fiqh by the restriction of 'amaliyya because they relate to the heart, not action. The evidence for this is that in Mala wa ma 'alayha it was said: if we want to exclude inner morals from fiqh, we would add the restriction of 'amal. So in the definition made by the Hanafis, inner morals are excluded by the restriction of 'amal; why are they not excluded from our definition? It seems the matn forgot that or did not see the restriction of 'amaliyya. Answer: What the matn said that the beauty of [؟], the ugliness of stinginess and arrogance are included in Sharia rulings, by that they do not mean the essence but their effects: i.e., the effect of stinginess is not giving obligatory charities — that is an action; the effect of generosity is giving voluntary charities; the effect of arrogance is not obeying Allah; the effect of humility is obeying those whose obedience Allah commanded. So when inner morals mean their effects, they are not excluded by the restriction of 'amal, so the definition of fiqh will not prevent the inclusion of others. His statement: 'And it is not added to it the terminology among the Shafi'is that knowledge of rulings is called fiqh only if it is obtained by way of nazar and istidlal, so that knowledge of the obligation of prayer and fasting and the like — which are well-known as necessary parts of religion such that the religious and others know them — is not counted as fiqh terminologically, and therefore they mention the restriction of iktisab and istidlal. So the Imam in al-Mahsul restricted the rulings to those not known as necessary parts of religion, and he said it is a precaution against knowledge of the obligation of prayer and fasting, for it is not called fiqh in the sense that it does not enter into the meaning of fiqh and is not counted from it, as he clarified in the restriction of 'amaliyya — not in the sense that if one does not take precaution against it, it would follow that the knower of merely their obligation is a faqih, as the author understood; so he objected by preventing that, based on the fact that the faqih is one who has fiqh, and fiqh is not knowledge of some rulings, even if few, so that one who knows one or two issues is a faqih; rather, one who knows a hundred strange istidlali issues alone is not called a faqih. Then, since their terminology is that knowledge of necessary matters of religion is not from fiqh, they must exclude them from their definition of fiqh; then the restriction that excludes them is not futile, and the statement that they are from fiqh is not correct according to them, and the terminology on that is not valid for objecting to them. Translation: Among the Shafi'is it is terminologically established that knowledge of rulings is called fiqh only when it is obtained by way of nazar and istidlal, so that knowledge of the obligation of prayer and fasting and the like, which have become well-known as from religion by necessity such that the religious and others know them, is not counted as fiqh terminologically; therefore they mention the restriction of iktisab and istidlal. And the Imam in al-Mahsul restricted rulings to those not known to be from religion by necessity, and he said it is a precaution against knowledge of the obligation of prayer and fasting, for it is not called fiqh in the sense that it does not enter into the name of fiqh and is not counted from it, as he clarified in the restriction of 'amaliyya — not in the sense that if he had not taken precaution, it would necessitate that the one who knows merely their obligation is a faqih, as the author understood; so he objected by preventing its necessity, based on the fact that the faqih is the one who possesses fiqh, and fiqh is not knowledge of some rulings even if few, so that one who knows one or two issues is a faqih; rather, one who knows a hundred strange istidlali issues alone is not called a faqih. Then, when their terminology is that knowledge of necessary matters of religion is not from fiqh, it was necessary to exclude them from the definition of fiqh; so the restriction that excludes them will not be futile, nor will the statement that they are from fiqh be correct according to them, nor is the terminology on that valid for objecting to them. Elaboration: His statement 'and it is not added to it': In this statement, the commentator objects to the matn, the detail of which is: some rulings are nazari and istidlali, i.e., dependent on evidence, and some are badihi, for which istidlal, nazar, and thought are not needed. Now the Shafi'is have established the terminology that for us fiqh is the name for nazari rulings, and knowledge of badihi rulings is not called fiqh, and every group has the right to establish terminology because the principle is 'la mushahata fi al-istilah'. Now when they have established the terminology...
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage is a dispute regarding the definition of fiqh between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools. The commentator (sharh) is explaining the matn's objection to the Shafi'i terminological addition. The Shafi'is define fiqh as knowledge of rulings obtained through nazar (reflection) and istidlal (deduction), thereby excluding necessary knowledge (daruriyyat) like the obligation of prayer and fasting. The Hanafi response, as presented here, argues first that the Hanafi definition itself excludes necessary knowledge through the requirement of detailed evidence (adilla tafsiliyya). Second, the commentator points out that even if one knows many istidlali rulings, that alone does not make one a faqih unless the knowledge is comprehensive. The passage also clarifies the earlier objection about inner morals (akhlaq batina): the matn's concern that such morals might enter the definition of fiqh is rebutted by stating that the referent is their external effects (athar), which are actions, thus they fall under the restriction of 'amaliyya. The overall conclusion is that each school's terminology is valid and not subject to objection ('la mushahata fi al-istilah').

Key Terms
النظر (al-nazar): Reflection and intellectual reasoning to derive a ruling from evidence
الاستدلال (al-istidlal): Deduction of a ruling from evidence after consideration
علم ضروري (ilm daruri): Necessary knowledge that is self-evident without proof
لا مشاحة في الاصطلاح (la mushahata fi al-istilah): There is no dispute over terminology; each school may define terms as they wish

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Shafi'i terminology mentioned in the passage, which of the following would NOT be considered fiqh?
A) Deriving the ruling of riba from a detailed hadith
B) Knowing the obligation of five daily prayers from the Qur'an without reasoning
C) Deriving the obligation of zakat through qiyas
D) Knowing the prohibition of interest through ijma' with analysis

Key Takeaway
Fiqh, as defined by Hanafis, is knowledge from detailed evidence; the Shafi'i requirement of nazar/istidlal is a valid terminological specification, not a substantive disagreement.

Defending the Definition of Fiqh Against Objections
Refuting objections to the definition of fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of fiqh and defend it against the four common objections using the linguistic distinction between collective and distributive 'kull'.

Core Idea
Fiqh is knowledge of all rulings in principle, not every specific answer.

This passage is a defence of the classical Hanafi definition of fiqh as 'knowledge of the shar'i rulings' (العلم بالأحكام الشرعية). The commentator addresses four objections that challenge this definition. Understanding how these objections are rebutted will sharpen your grasp of what it truly means to be a faqih in the Hanafi tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition under attack
Recognise that the definition being defended is that fiqh is knowledge of all the shar'i rulings (كُلّ الْأَحْكَام). The objections arise from misunderstanding the scope of 'all'.
2
List the four objections
The four objections are: (1) rulings are non-finite, so no one can know all; (2) even a consensus faqih like Imam Malik may not know many rulings; (3) 'most' rulings is also unknown; (4) knowing one or two rulings would make one a faqih.
3
Analyse the rebuttals
The commentator responds: (1) 'all' means the totality in principle, not each future event; (2) Malik's ignorance does not negate his comprehensive capacity; (3) the definition uses 'all' not 'most'; (4) fiqh requires knowledge of the whole body, not isolated parts.
The key is the distinction between 'كُلّ' as collective whole vs. 'كُلُّ وَاحِدٍ' as each individual.
4
Understand the linguistic analysis of 'kull'
The commentator shows that 'all rulings' can be understood collectively (the whole) or distributively (each one). In this definition, 'all' refers to the collective totality of rulings, not the individual details.
Examples: 'all the people lift this stone' (collective) vs. 'each individual is fed by this food' (distributive).
5
Synthesise the corrected definition
Fiqh is the knowledge of the entire body of shar'i rulings in a principled, comprehensive manner, which is compatible with not knowing every specific detail instantly.
This reconciles the non-finiteness of events with the comprehensive capacity of a mujtahid.

Source Text

صَابِطَ يَجْمَعُهَا لِاخْتِلَافِ الْحَوَادِثِ اخْتِلَافًا لَا يَدْخُلُ تَحْتَ الضَّبْطِ فَلا يَكُونُ أَحَدٌ فَقِيها . وَأَمَّا الثَّانِي فَلَانَّ بَعْضَ مَنْ هُوَ فَقِيهٌ بِالْإِجْمَاعِ قَدْ لَا يَعْرِفُ بَعْضَ الْأَحْكَامِ كَمَالِكٍ سُئِلَ عَنْ أَرْبَعِينَ مَسْأَلَةً فَقَالَ فِي سِتٍ وَثَلَاثِينَ لَا أَدْرِي . وَأَمَّا الثَّالِثُ فَلَانَّ الْكُلَّ مَجْهُولُ الْكَمِّيَّةِ وَالْجَهْلُ بِكَمِّيَّةِ الْكُلِّ يَسْتَلْزِمُ الْجَهْلَ بِكَمِّيَّةِ الْكُسُورِ الْمُضَافَةِ إِلَيْهِ مِنْ النِّصْفِ وَغَيْرِهِ ضَرُورَةً وَبِهَذَا يَظْهَرُ أَنَّهُ لَا يَصِحُ أَنْ يُرَادَ أَكْثَرُ الْأَحْكَامِ ; لِأَنَّهُ عِبَارَةٌ عَمَّا فَوْقَ النِّصْفِ وَهُوَ أَيْضًا مَجْهُولٌ . وَأَمَّا الرَّابِعُ فَلَانَّهُ يَسْتَلْزِمُ أَنْ يَكُونَ الْعَالِمُ بِمَسْأَلَةٍ أَوْ مَسْأَلَتَيْنِ مِنْ الدَّلِيلِ فَقِيهًا وَلَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ اصْطِلَاحًا . وَهَذَا مَذْكُورٌ فِيمَا سَبَقَ فَلَمْ يُصَرِّحْ بِهِ هَهُنَا بَلْ أَشَارَ إِلَيْهِ بِلَفْظِ ثُمَّ أَيْ بَعْدَمَا لَا يُرَادُ الْبَعْضُ وَإِنْ قَلَّ لَا يُرَادُ الْكُلُّ إِلَى آخِرِهِ . وَهَهُنَا بَحْثٌ وَهُوَ أَنَّ مِنْ الْأَحْكَامِ مَا يَصِحُّ حَمْلُهُ عَلَى الْكُلِّ دُونَ كُلِّ وَاحِدٍ كَقَوْلِنَا كُلُّ الْقَوْمِ يَرْفَعُ هَذَا الْحَجَرَ لَا كُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمْ وَمِنْهَا مَا هُوَ بِالْعَكْسِ كَقَوْلِنَا كُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْ النَّاسِ يَكْفِيهِ هَذَا الطَّعَامُ لَا كُلُّ النَّاسِ وَمِنْهَا مَا لَا يَخْتَلِفُ كَقَوْلِنَا ضَرَبْتُ كُلَّ الْقَوْمِ أَوْ كُلَّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمْ . وَمَعْرِفَةُ الْأَحْكَامِ مِنْ هَذَا الْقَبِيلِ إِذْ مَعْرِفَةُ جَمِيعِ الْأَحْكَامِ مَعْرِفَةُ كُلِّ حُكْمٍ وَبِالْعَكْسِ . وَأَنَّهُ الْتَزَمَ الْمُصَنِّفُ أَنَّ مَعْرِفَةَ جَمِيعِ الْأَحْكَامِ أَعَمُّ مِنْ مَعْرِفَةِ كُلِّ وَاحِدٍ أَوْ الْبَعْضِ فَقَطْ فَعَدَمُ تَنَاهِي الْحَوَادِثِ لَا يُنَافِي ذَلِكَ . وَالظَّاهِرُ أَنَّهُ قَصَدَ بِالْكُلِّ مَجْمُوعَ الْأَحْكَامِ الْمَاضِيَةِ وَالْآتِيَةِ وَبِكُلِّ وَاحِدٍ مَا يَقَعُ وَيَدْخُلُ فِي الْوُجُودِ عَلَى التَّفْصِيلِ وَيَلْتَفِتُ إِلَيْهِ ذِهَنُ الْمُجْتَهِدِ . جَيْتُ عَلَّلَ عَدَمَ إِرَادَةِ الْأَوَّلِ بِلَا تَنَاهِي الْحَوَادِثِ وَالثَّانِي بِقَوْلِ لَا أَدْرِي [؟]
A controller that gathers them due to the intense difference of events in a way that does not come under control, so no one is a faqih. As for the second: because some who is a faqih by consensus may not know some rulings, like Malik who was asked about forty questions and said for thirty-six 'I do not know'. As for the third: because the whole is unknown in quantity, and ignorance of the quantity of the whole necessarily entails ignorance of the quantity of the fractions added to it like half and others. By this it appears that it is not valid to intend 'most of the rulings' because it is an expression for what is above half, and it is also unknown. As for the fourth: because it entails that one who knows one or two issues from the evidence is a faqih, but it is not like that terminologically. And this has been mentioned previously, so he did not explicitly state it here, but rather alluded to it with the word 'thumma' i.e., after it is not intended the part, even if small, and not intended the whole, etc. And here is a discussion: some rulings can be applied to the whole without each individual, like our saying 'all the people lift this stone' not each one of them; and some are opposite, like our saying 'each individual among the people this food suffices him' not all the people; and some do not differ, like our saying 'I struck all the people' or 'each one of them'. And knowledge of rulings is of this type, since knowledge of all rulings is knowledge of each ruling and vice versa. And the author committed that knowledge of all rulings is more general than knowledge of each individual or merely some, so the non-finiteness of events does not contradict that. The apparent is that he intended by 'all' the totality of past and future rulings, and by 'each individual' what occurs and comes into existence in detail and to which the mind of the mujtahid turns. He [؟] explained the non-intention of the first by the non-finiteness of events and the second by the statement 'I do not know'.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage is a commentary defending the definition of fiqh as 'knowledge of the shar'i rulings' against four objections. The commentator (likely from Al-Talwih) explains that the definition does not require a faqih to know every single ruling that will ever occur. The first objection claimed that since events are non-finite, knowing all rulings is impossible. The response is that 'all' here means the totality of rulings in principle, not each specific future event. The second objection cited Imam Malik's admission of ignorance on many questions, arguing that he would not be a faqih if fiqh required knowing all. The response is that Malik was still a faqih by consensus; knowing all rulings does not mean knowing every answer instantly, but having the capacity to derive them. The third objection argued that 'most' rulings is also unknown. The response shows that the definition does not use 'most', but 'all' in a technical sense. The fourth objection claimed that knowing one or two rulings would make one a faqih. The response clarifies that the definition requires knowledge that is comprehensive in principle, not mere isolated rulings. The commentator then discusses the linguistic distinction between 'all' (kull) applying collectively versus distributively, giving examples. He concludes that the author's definition is sound because it means knowledge of the entire body of rulings in a principled way, not each detail. The authority weight of this discussion is from the classical Hanafi usul tradition, as it is a commentary on Al-Tawdih war Al-Talwih, which are standard texts.

Key Terms
فَقِيه (faqih): One who has comprehensive knowledge of shar'i rulings such that he can derive them from evidence, not necessarily knowing every single ruling instantly.
كُلّ الْأَحْكَام (kull al-ahkam): The totality of shar'i rulings in principle, encompassing both past and future, not each individual ruling as it occurs in detail.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because a faqih only needs to know past rulings, not future ones.
B) Because 'all' refers to the total body of rulings in principle, not each future event.
C) Because the definition actually uses 'most' not 'all'.
D) Because non-finite events are not a problem for divine knowledge.

Key Takeaway
A faqih is defined by comprehensive capacity to derive rulings, not by instant knowledge of every detail.

Refuting Invalid Interpretations of the Scope of Fiqh
Refuting four possibilities for the scope of fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the four invalid interpretations of the scope of fiqh and explain the Hanafi reason for rejecting each based on the nature of hawadith.

Core Idea
Fiqh cannot mean knowing all rulings nor a fixed portion, because incidents are countless and beyond human grasp.

The definition of fiqh as 'knowledge of rulings' raises immediate questions: does this mean every single ruling? Most rulings? Some specific portion? The Hanafi scholars rigorously examine each possibility and find all but one fatally flawed. In this lesson, we walk through their reasoning, which hinges on the nature of hawadith (novel events) and the limits of human capacity.

Guided Path
1
State the four possibilities
The matn author considers four possibilities for what 'knowledge of rulings' might mean: (1) the collective whole (kull majmu'i), (2) the individual whole (kull afradi), (3) a specified portion (e.g., half, most), and (4) an indefinite few. List them clearly.
These correspond to different interpretations of 'all rulings' in the definition.
2
Examine the refutation of each possibility
For each possibility, the author provides a specific argument why it is invalid. Understand the reasoning: the first is impossible because incidents are too numerous and varied to be encompassed; the second because even great scholars (like Imam Malik) do not know every ruling; the third because the whole is unknown so its fractions are also unknown; the fourth would make one who knows a few rulings a faqih, which contradicts terminology.
The talil (causal reasoning) is key: each refutation relies on the inherent nature of hawadith.
3
Distinguish kull majmu'i and kull afradi
The commentary introduces the distinction between collective whole and individual whole. Understand that kull majmu'i refers to the total set of rulings as one aggregate, while kull afradi refers to knowledge of each individual ruling as it occurs. This distinction is crucial for the first two refutations.
The author uses this distinction to show that neither is attainable.
4
Recognize the practical limit of human knowledge
The example of Imam Malik not knowing 36 out of 40 issues illustrates that even the most knowledgeable scholars cannot know every ruling. This supports the rejection of kull afradi.
This is not a criticism of Imam Malik but a demonstration of the nature of fiqh.
5
Conclude the positive implication
Although the passage refutes definitions, it implicitly leads to the conclusion that fiqh is not about knowing a fixed set of rulings but about the ability to derive rulings through usul. This sets the stage for the proper definition.
The refutation clears the ground for the accepted definition of fiqh as knowledge of practical legal rulings derived from detailed evidence.

جو فقیہ (مجتہد ) ہے اجماع کے ساتھ کبھی نہیں پہچان سکتا بعض احکام کو جیسے امام مالک سے سوال کیا گیا چالیس مسئلوں کے بارہ میں پس فرمایا انہوں نے چھتیس کے بارہ میں لا ادری۔ اور بہر حال تیسرا اس لئے کہ کل مجہول الكمية ( مقدار) ہے اور کل کی مقدار کی جہالت مستلزم ہوتی ہے کسور کی مقدار کی جہالت کو جن کی اضافت کی گئی ہو اس کی طرف نصف سے اور اس کے علاوہ سے ضرورۃ اس سے ظاہر ہو جاتی ہے یہ بات کہ نہیں ہے صحیح یہ کہ مراد لیا جائے اکثر احکام کو اس لئے وہ عبادت ہوتے ہیں ان احکام سے جو نصف سے اوپر ہیں اور وہ بھی مجہول ہیں ۔ اور بہر حال رابع اس لئے کہ وہ مستلزم ہوتا ہے اس بات کو کہ ایک مسئلہ یا دو مسکلوں کو دلیل سے جانے والا بھی فقیہ بن جائے اور نہیں ہے اس طرح اصطلاحاً۔ اور یہ مذکور ہے ماقبل میں پس نہیں تصریح کی گئی اس کی یہاں بلکہ اشارہ کیا ہے اسکی طرف ٹم کے لفظ کے ساتھ یعنی بعد ا سکے کہ نہیں مراد لیا الحجر لاكل واحد منهم اور بعض ان میں وہ ہیں جو اس کے برعکس ہیں جیسے ہمارا قول كل واحد من الناس يكفيه هذا الطعام لاكل الناس اور بعض ان میں سے وہ ہیں جو مختلف نہیں ہوتے جیسے ہمارا قول ضربت كل القوم او كل واحد منہم اور احکام کا پہچاننا اسی قبیل سے ہے اس لئے کہ جمیع احکام کا پہچانا ہر ہر حکم کا پہچاننا ہے اور عکس کے ساتھ اور تحقیق التزام کیا مصنف نے اس بات کا کہ جمیع احکام کا پہچاننا ائم ہے کل واحد کے پہچاننے سے یا بعض کے پہچاننے سے فقط پس حوادثات کا متنا ہی نہ ہونا نہیں ہے منافی اس کے اور ظاہر ہے یہ بات کہ ارادہ کیا گیا کل کے ساتھ مجموعہ احکام ماضیہ اور آئنیہ کا اور کل واحد کے ساتھ ان کا جو واقع ہو چکے اور داخل ہو چکے وجود میں اور پہنچتا ہے اسکی طرف مجتہدین کا ذہن اس حیثیت سے کہ اول کے عدم ارادے کی علت بیان کی حوادثات کے غیر متناہی ہونے کے ساتھ اور ثانی کا ثبوت لا ادری کے ساتھ۔

متن کی وضاحت
تشریح : قولہ ثم اعلم :۔ یہ شرح کی عبارت کا پہلا حصہ ہے غرض شارع متقن کی وضاحت ہے فقہ کی تعریف پر ماتن نے اعتراض کیا تھا اس کی وضاحت کرنا مقصود ہے اعتراض سمجھنے سے قبل ایک مقدمہ کا جاننا ضروری ہے اور وہ یہ کہ اگر جمع کا صیغہ ذکر کیا جائے اور اس کے سارے افراد کا مجموعہ مراد لیا جائے یا تفصیل اس کی انواع میں سے ہر ہر نوع کے تمام افراد پر اس کا حکم لگایا جائے تو یہ کل مجموعی کہلاتا ہے اور اگر اس جمع کی جزئیات میں اس کی ہر ہر جزئی پر وقوع بعد وقوع کے اعتبار سے حکم لگایا جائے تو کل افرادی کہلاتا ہے۔ اب اعتراض سمجھیں : اعتراض یہ تھا کہ احکام سے مراد کیا ہے مجموعہ احکام مراد ہیں یعنی بعثت نبوی صلى الله عليه وسلم سے لیکر قیامت تک ان تمام احکام کے جانے کا نام فقہ ہے جو حاصل ہے کل مجموعی کا یا کل واحد کے جاننے کا نام فقہ ہے جو حاصل ہے کل افرادی کا یا بعض احکام کے جاننے کا نام فقہ ہے وہ بعض متعین ہوں کل کی طرف نسبت کرتے ہوئے یعنی کل کا نصف ہوں یا مثلث ہوں یا اکثر ہوں یا بعض غیر متعین ہوں یہ چاروں احتمال باطل میں پہلا احتال تو اس لئے باطل ہے احکام کا مجموع من حیث المجموع ہونا اگر چہ ذات کے اعتبار سے متناہی ہیں دنیا کے ختم ہونے کی وجہ سے لیکن چونکہ حوادثات کثیر ہیں اس کثرت کی وجہ سے حکماً غیر متناہی ہیں اور اس کو معلوم کرنے کی دو صورتیں ہیں (۱) ہر ہر جزئی کا علم حاصل ہو (۲) مجموعہ احکام جو حکماً غیر متناہی ہیں ان کا اجمالی علم حاصل ہو کسی ضابطے کی وجہ سے تو پہلی صورت بشر کے احاطہ میں نہیں دوسری صورت بھی ممکن نہیں ضابطے کے نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے یعنی کوئی ایسا ضابطہ نہیں پایا جا سکتا جو تمام حوادث اور مسائل کو جامع ہو کیونکہ حوادثات مختلف واقع ہوتے ہیں ۔ دوسرا احتمال یعنی کل افرادی بھی مراد نہیں لے سکتے کیونکہ ایسے لوگ جو یقیناً فقیہ ہیں ان سے سوال کیا گیا مگر وہ جواب نہ دے سکے جیسے امام مالک سے چالیس (مسائل) کے بارہ میں سوال ہوا تو چھتیں کے جواب میں کہالا ادری تیسرا احتمال یعنی بعض متعیین منسوب الی الکل بھی مراد نہیں لے سکتے کیونکہ کل مجبول ہے میستلزم ہے اس کے کسور کے مجبول ہونے کو ۔ اس سے یہ بھی معلوم ہو گیا کہ اکثر احکام بھی مراد نہیں لے سکتے کیونکہ جب کل احکام مجہول ہیں تو اس کا نصف بھی مجبول ہوگا تو اکثر بھی مجبول ہونگے کیونکہ اکثر مافوق انصف کو کہتے ہیں۔ چوتھا احتمال یعنی بعض غیر متعین احکام بھی مراد نہیں لے سکتے ورنہ تو ایک دو یا تین مسائل دلیل سے جانے والا فقیہ ہو گا اس پر سوال نہوا۔
Some intend a specific portion related to the whole, like half, or most, or something, or else some absolutely, even if few. And all types are invalid. In the first case, because incidents, although finite in themselves with the end of the abode of obligation (the world), yet they are numerous and continuous as long as the world exists, they are not under the control of present individuals or the grasp of mujtahids. That is the meaning of his saying 'لا تنکا د تمنا ہی', they cannot be known in their particular rulings one by one due to human inability to encompass them, nor collectively with detail because there is no rule that can collect all these rulings due to the severe difference of incidents, a difference that cannot enter under any control. Therefore, there will not be a single faqih. And in the second case, because some who are faqih (mujtahid) by consensus sometimes cannot recognize some rulings, as Imam Malik was asked about forty issues and he said 'I do not know' for thirty-six. And in the third case, because the whole is unknown in quantity, and ignorance of the quantity of the whole necessitates ignorance of the quantity of its fractions to which it is related by half and other than half necessarily. Thus it becomes clear that it is not correct to intend most rulings, for they are [عبادت?] rulings above half and they are also unknown. And in the fourth case, because it would entail that one who knows one or two issues by proof becomes a faqih, and that is not the case terminologically. And this was mentioned previously, so it is not explicitly stated here but indicated by the word 'تم' meaning after it cannot be intended some even if few, nor can it be intended the whole etc. And here is a discussion: some rulings are such that acting upon them collectively is valid unlike each individual, like our saying 'All the people lift this stone' for each one of them. And some are opposite, like our saying 'This food suffices every person' for all people. And some are not different, like our saying 'I struck all the people' or 'each one of them'. And knowing rulings is of this type because knowing all rulings is knowing each ruling and vice versa. And the author verified the entailment that knowing all rulings is from knowing each one or from knowing some only. Thus incidents not being finite does not contradict this. And it is clear that by 'whole' is intended the sum of past and future rulings, and by 'each one' those that have occurred and entered existence, and the minds of mujtahids reach it from the aspect that the cause of not intending the first is stated by the non-finiteness of incidents, and the proof of the second by 'I do not know'.

Explanation of the text: Commentary: His saying 'then know'. This is the first part of the commentary phrase. The purpose is to explain the precise commentator's clarification of the definition of fiqh that the matn author objected to. Before understanding the objection, a premise is necessary: if the plural form is mentioned and the sum of all its individuals is intended, or the rule is applied to all individuals of each type in detail, this is called 'kull majmu'i' (collective whole). And if the rule is applied to each individual part of that plural in succession, it is called 'kull afradi' (individual whole). Now understand the objection: The objection was: what is meant by rulings? Is it the sum of rulings from the time of the prophetic mission to the Day of Judgment, so that fiqh is knowledge of that collective whole? Or is it knowledge of each individual ruling, which is the individual whole? Or is it knowledge of some rulings, those specific portions related to the whole, i.e., half, or one-third, or most? Or some indefinite rulings? All four possibilities are invalid. The first possibility is invalid because the sum of rulings as a whole, although finite in essence due to the end of the world, is legally non-finite due to the multitude of incidents. Knowing it has two forms: (1) knowledge of each particular, (2) knowledge of the totality of rulings, which are legally non-finite, through some principle. The first form is beyond human capacity; the second is also impossible due to the lack of a principle—no principle can encompass all incidents and issues because incidents occur differently. The second possibility, i.e., the individual whole, cannot be intended because people who are definitely fuqaha were asked and could not answer, like Imam Malik was asked about forty issues and said 'I do not know' for thirty-six. The third possibility, i.e., specific portions related to the whole, cannot be intended because the whole is unknown, necessitating ignorance of its fractions. Hence most rulings cannot be intended because when the whole is unknown, its half is unknown, so most is also unknown, as 'most' means above half. The fourth possibility, i.e., indefinite some rulings, cannot be intended, else one who knows one or two issues by proof would be a faqih, and that is not the case. On this there is no objection.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion on the definition of fiqh, specifically refuting various interpretations of what 'knowledge of rulings' means. The author systematically eliminates four possibilities: 1) knowledge of the entire set of rulings collectively (kull majmu'i), 2) knowledge of each individual ruling (kull afradi), 3) knowledge of a specified portion of rulings (e.g., half or most), and 4) knowledge of an indefinite few rulings. The reasoning is grounded in the nature of incidents (hawadith): although finite in themselves, they are countless in practice and cannot be fully encompassed by any human. The example of Imam Malik not knowing answers to many questions demonstrates that even great scholars do not know every ruling, thus fiqh cannot mean knowledge of all individual rulings. The impossibility of knowing the whole implies impossibility of knowing fractions. The fourth possibility would make anyone who knows a few issues a faqih, which contradicts the accepted definition. The author clarifies that the intended meaning of 'whole' here is the totality of past and future rulings, and 'each one' refers to rulings that have actually occurred. The discussion is essential for establishing that fiqh is not all-encompassing knowledge but rather a learned expertise within human capacity.

Key Terms
کل مجموعی (kull majmu'i): The collective whole: knowledge of all rulings as a single aggregate from prophetic mission to Day of Judgment.
کل افرادی (kull afradi): The individual whole: knowledge of each and every ruling as individually occurring.
حوادثات (hawadith): Newly occurring events or incidents that require legal rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the first possibility (knowledge of the collective whole of all rulings) invalid?
A) Because the total number of rulings is finite and known.
B) Because incidents are so numerous and varied that no human can encompass them all, and there is no single rule covering all.
C) Because Imam Malik said 'I do not know' for many issues.
D) Because knowing the whole would require knowing fractions, which is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of fiqh excludes exhaustive knowledge; it is about the ability to derive rulings via usul from evidence, not memorizing every rule.

The Hanafi Definition of Fiqh: Knowledge with Istinbat Faculty
Defining fiqh as combined knowledge of revealed and consensual rulings with the faculty of derivation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of fiqh, explain why it requires both knowledge of revealed/ijma'i rulings and the faculty of istinbat, and respond to the objection that fiqh is zanni.

Core Idea
Fiqh is not just knowing rulings—it is the active faculty to derive them correctly from revelation and consensus.

In this passage, the author refines the definition of fiqh. He insists that fiqh is knowledge (ʿilm) but not merely memorization; it requires a faculty (malakah) for sound derivation. He also defends calling fiqh 'ilm despite its zanni aspects by giving three arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defined term and its genus
The author defines fiqh as a type of 'ilm (knowledge). Identify the genus (ʿilm) and the differentia: it is knowledge of a finite, determined totality of practical Sharʿi rulings.
The definition excludes unlimited and unknown matters.
2
Break down the two components of the definition
The definition has two parts: (a) knowledge of rulings from clear revelation or consensus, and (b) the faculty (malakah) of sound istinbat from those sources. These are both necessary.
The istinbat faculty distinguishes a faqih from a mere memorizer.
3
Understand the justification for requiring istinbat faculty
The author argues that requiring knowledge of all qiyasi issues would cause circularity (dawr). Instead, the faculty to derive them is sufficient. He also notes that Companions were fuqaha even before all rulings were revealed because they had this faculty.
Circularity: if fiqh is defined as knowing qiyasi issues, and qiyasi issues are derived using fiqh, the definition would presuppose itself.
4
Analyze the three responses to the 'zanni' objection
Objection: fiqh is often zanni, so how can it be called 'ilm (certain knowledge)? Three answers: (1) The core revealed and ijmaʿi rulings are certain. (2) The word 'ilm is applied to zanni matters too (e.g., medicine). (3) Since the Lawgiver considers preponderant conjecture (ghalabat al-zann), the establishment of a ruling becomes effectively certain for the mujtahid, though the path is zanni.
The third answer depends on the view that every mujtahid is correct (taswib). The text begins to address the opposite view (takhti'a) but is cut off.
5
Note the incomplete dispute on taswib/takhti'a
The passage acknowledges that the third answer is only valid according to taswib. The full response for takhti'a is not given, indicating a point for further study.
This is an important usul dispute: are all mujtahids correct (taswib) or is only one correct (takhti'a)? The Hanafi position is generally takhti'a in fiqh, but the passage uses taswib to strengthen the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا عَرَفتَ هَذَا فَلا بُدَّ أن يَكُونَ الفِقهُ عِلمًا بِجُمْلَةٍ مُتَنَاهِيَةٍ مَصْبُوطَةٍ فَلِهَذَا قَالَ بَل هُوَ العِلمُ بكُلِّ الأحكام الشَّرعِيَّةِ العَمَلِيَّةِ الَّتِي قَد ظَهَرَ نُزُولُ الوَحى بها وَالَّتِي انعَقَدَ الإجمَاعُ عَلَيْهَا مِن أَدِلَّتِهَا مَعَ مَلَكَةِ الاستِنبَاطِ الصَّحِيحِ مِنْهَا ) فَالمُعتَبَرُ أَن يُعْلَمَ فِي أَي وَقتٍ كَانَ جَمِيعَ مَا قَد ظَهَرَ نُزُولُ الوَحِي بِهِ فِي ذَلِكَ الوَقتِ فَالصَّحَابَةُ رَضِيَ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى عَنهُم كَانُوا فُقَهَاءَ فِي وَقتِ نُزُولِ بَعض الأحكامِ بَعدَهُ ثُمَّ مَا لَم يَظْهَر نُزُولُ الوَحِي بِهِ قَد لَا يَعْلَمُهُ الفَقِيهُ وَالصَّحَابَةُ رَضِيَ اللَّهُ عَنهُم لِعَرَبِيَّتِهِم كَانُوا عَالِمِينَ بِمَا ذُكِرَ وَلَم يُطلَق الفَقِيهُ إِلَّا عَلَى المُستَنبِطِينَ مِنهُم وَعِلمُ المَسائل الإجْمَاعِيَّةِ يُشتَرَطُ إِلَّا فِى زَمَن رَسُول الله صَلَّى اللهُ عَلَيهِ وَسَلَّمَ لِعَدَمِ الإِجْمَاعِ فِي زَمَنِهِ لَا المَسَائِلُ القِيَاسِيَّةُ لِلدَّورِ بَل يُشترَطُ مَلَكَةُ الاستنبَاطِ الصَّحِيحِ وَهُوَ أَن يَكُونَ مَقرُونَا بِشَرَايْطِهِ وَمَا قِيلَ : إِنَّ الفِقَهَ ظَنِّى فَلِمَ أطْلِقَ العِلمَ عَلَيْهِ فَجَوَابُهُ أَوَّلًا أَنَّهُ مَقطوع بهِ فَإِنَّ الجُمْلَةَ الَّتِي ذَكَرنَا أَنَّهَا فِقهُ وَهِيَ مَا قَد ظَهَرَ نُزُولُ الوَحِي بِهِ وَمَا انعَقَدَ الإِجْمَاعُ عَلَيْهِ قَطِعِيَّةٌ وَثَانِيا : أَنَّ العِلمَ يُطلَقُ عَلَى الظَّنِّيَّاتِ كَمَا يُطلَقُ عَلَى القَطِعِيَّاتِ كَالطَّبٌ وَنَحوِهِ وَثَالِفًا أَنَّ الشَّارِعَ لَمَّا اعْتَبَرَ غَلَبَةَ الظَّنِّ فِي الأحكام صَارَ كَأَنَّهُ قَالَ كُلَّمَا غَلَبَ ظَنُّ المُجتَهِدِ بِالحُكمِ يَثبُتُ الحُكمُ فَكُلَّمَا وُجِدَ غَلَبَهُ ظَنِّ المُجْتَهِدِ يَكُونُ ثُبوت الحكمِ مَقطُوعًا بِهِ وَالظَّنُّ فِى طَرِيْقِهِ فَهَذَا الجَوَابُ عَلَى مَذهَبِ مَن يَقُولُ إِنَّ كُلَّ مُجتَهِد مُصِيبٌ يَكُونُ صَحِيحًا وَأَمَّا عِندَ مَن لَا يَقُولُ بِهِ فَيُرَادُ بِقَولِهِ كُلَّمَا
And when you know this, it is necessary that fiqh be knowledge of a finite, determined totality. Therefore he (the author) said: 'Rather, it is knowledge of all the practical Shar'i rulings that have been revealed by revelation and those upon which ijma' (consensus) has been reached, from their evidences, along with the faculty (malakah) of sound istinbat from them.' So what is considered is that one knows, at any time, all that has been revealed in that time. So the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) were jurists (fuqaha) at the time of the revelation of some rulings after it. Then, what has not been revealed by revelation, the faqih may not know. The Companions, due to their Arabic, knew what was mentioned, and the term 'faqih' was only applied to those among them who performed istinbat. Knowledge of ijma'i issues is required except in the time of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) due to the absence of ijma' in his time. Qiyasi issues are not required due to circularity (dawr). Rather, the faculty of sound istinbat is required, which is that it be accompanied by its conditions. And what is said: 'Fiqh is zanni (speculative), so why is the term 'ilm (knowledge) applied to it?' The answer is: First, it is certain (qat'i) because the totality we mentioned (revealed and ijma'i rulings) is certain. Second, 'ilm is applied to zanniyyat as it is to qat'iyyat, like medicine and similar fields. Third, since the Lawgiver (Shari) has considered preponderant conjecture (ghalabat al-zann) in rulings, it becomes as if He said: Whenever the mujtahid's conjecture about a ruling is preponderant, the ruling is established. So whenever preponderant conjecture of the mujtahid exists, the establishment of the ruling is certain (qat'i), while the conjecture lies in its path. So this answer is correct according to the view that every mujtahid is correct. But according to the one who does not hold that, it is meant by his saying 'whenever'... (incomplete).
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage defines fiqh as a specific kind of knowledge. The definition includes two components: (1) knowledge of practical Shar'i rulings that come from clear revelation or have achieved ijma'; and (2) the faculty (malakah) to perform sound istinbat (derivation) from those sources. The author clarifies that fiqh is not just knowing any rulings, but a finite set that changes with time: Companions knew only the rulings revealed in their era, yet they were fuqaha because they had the istinbat faculty. The knowledge of ijma'i issues is required for a faqih, but qiyasi issues are not required because that would cause circularity (dawr) — if fiqh requires knowledge of qiyasi issues, and qiyasi issues are derived from fiqh, it would be circular. Instead, the istinbat faculty itself is required. The passage then addresses an objection: fiqh is often zanni (speculative), so why call it 'ilm (certain knowledge)? Three answers: (1) The core rulings (revealed and ijma'i) are certain. (2) The word 'ilm is sometimes used for zanni matters, like medicine. (3) On the view that every mujtahid is correct (taswib), the establishment of a ruling via ghalabat al-zann is effectively certain in practice, though the path is zanni. The last sentence begins to address the opposite view (takhti'a) but is cut off.

Key Terms
الفقه (al-fiqh): Knowledge of practical Shar'i rulings from revelation and ijma', together with the faculty of sound istinbat
ملکة الاستنباط الصحيح (malakat al-istinbat al-sahih): The faculty of sound derivation, i.e., the ability to correctly extract rulings from their sources while meeting all conditions

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is fiqh called 'ilm (knowledge) even though many of its rulings are zanni?
A) Fiqh consists only of certain rulings from Quran and Mutawatir Hadith.
B) The term 'ilm is applied to zanniyyat as well, and the Lawgiver's consideration of ghalabat al-zann makes the ruling effectively certain.
C) Fiqh is not zanni; all its rulings are certain.
D) The definition only applies to rulings from ijma', which are certain.

Key Takeaway
Fiqh is knowledge of certain (revealed/ijma'i) rulings combined with the faculty of sound derivation; its zanni aspects do not disqualify it from being called 'ilm.

The Third Definition of Fiqh: Scope and Justification
Defines fiqh and defends its classification as knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the third definition of fiqh, explain why qiyasi rulings are excluded, and evaluate the objection that fiqh involves zann.

Core Idea
Fiqh is certain knowledge of revealed and consensus-based rulings, excluding qiyas to avoid circularity.

Defining fiqh precisely is a foundational task in usul al-fiqh. The third definition, proposed by Sadr al-Shariah, attempts to resolve tensions in earlier definitions by limiting the scope to rulings from revelation and consensus, and by including the faculty of correct inference (malakat al-istinbat). This lesson unpacks that definition, the reason for excluding qiyas, and the defense of calling fiqh 'ilm despite the prevalence of zann.

Guided Path
1
Deconstruct the definition
Identify the four components: ilm (knowledge), practical shar‘i rulings, sourced from revelation or consensus, and the faculty of correct inference.
The definition is carefully crafted to exclude qiyas-based rulings.
2
Understand the exclusion of qiyas
Qiyasi rulings are excluded because including them would lead to circularity (dawr): fiqh would depend on qiyas, and qiyas depends on fiqh.
This ensures epistemic coherence.
3
Analyze the objection
If fiqh is often based on zann (speculation), how can it be called ilm (certain knowledge)?
This objection must be addressed to preserve the definition.
4
Evaluate the three responses
Three answers: 1) The included rulings are qat‘i; 2) ‘ilm is applied loosely to speculative fields; 3) Ghalabat al-zann is treated as certainty in practice.
Each response offers a different angle to reconcile zann with ilm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هُوَ الْعِلْمُ بِكُلِ الاحْكامِ الشَّرْعِيَّةِ العَمَلِيَّةِ اللَّتِي قَدْ ظَهَرَ نُزُولُ الْوَحْيِ بِهَا وَاللَّتِي انْعَقَدَ الْإِجْمَاعُ عَلَيْهَا مِنْ اَدِلَّتِهَا مَعَ مَلَكَةِ الْاسْتِنْبَاطِ الصَّحِيحِ مِنْهَا
It is the knowledge of all the practical legal rulings whose revelation has become apparent and upon which consensus has been formed, from their evidences, along with the faculty of correct inference from them.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage presents the third definition of fiqh, proposed by the author of the matn (Sadr al-Shariah) to be comprehensive and precise. fiqh is defined as knowledge ('ilm) of all practical shar'i rulings that have been revealed by revelation (Qur'an and Sunnah) or established by consensus (ijma), acquired through one's proofs (adillah) with the faculty (malakah) of correct istinbat (extraction). This definition excludes qiyasi (analogical) rulings because they are not directly from revelation or consensus; they are products of the jurist's own inference. The inclusion of 'malakat al-istinbat al-sahih' ensures that only those who possess the ability to derive rulings correctly—like the Sahaba who were Arabs but not all were fuqaha—are considered jurists. The passage then addresses an objection: fiqh is often based on zann (speculation) due to qiyas, so how can it be called 'ilm (certain knowledge)? Three answers are given: First, the rulings in question (from revelation and consensus) are certain (qat'i). Second, 'ilm is loosely applied to speculative fields like medicine. Third, according to the view that every mujtahid is correct (musib), the dominant conjecture (ghalabat al-zann) effectively makes the ruling certain; for those who disagree, it means the ruling is binding in practice or established with respect to the evidence, even if not in God's eternal knowledge. The definition carefully avoids circularity (dawr) by not including qiyasi rulings, as that would make fiqh dependent on itself.

Key Terms
الفقه (al-fiqh): Knowledge (ilm) of all practical shar'i rulings that are based on revelation and consensus, acquired through correct inference (istinbat).
مَلَكَةُ الإستنباط (malakat al-istinbāt): A firmly rooted faculty in the jurist's mind enabling correct derivation of rulings from their sources.
ظَنّ (ẓann): Probable conjecture that does not reach certainty; many fiqh rulings are based on it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the third definition in this passage, why are qiyasi rulings not considered part of fiqh?
A) Because they are not practical rulings.
B) Because including them would lead to circularity (dawr).
C) Because they are not based on revelation or consensus.
D) Because they are always speculative (zanni) and thus cannot be knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The third definition of fiqh limits its scope to revelation and consensus to avoid circularity, and defends the label 'ilm through multiple arguments addressing the role of zann.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

The Hanafi Definition of Fiqh: Certainty of Qiyas and the Faculty of Istinbat
Definition of fiqh with conditions and response to objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of fiqh, identify its conditions, and explain why qiyasi rulings are considered certain within the madhhab.

Core Idea
Fiqh is certain knowledge of Shar'i rulings, including qiyas, because Shari'ah elevates predominant opinion to binding certainty.

This passage from Al-Talwih and its Urdu sharh tackles a critical question: Is fiqh certain (qat'i) or probabilistic (zanni)? The Hanafi answer secures the epistemological foundation of the discipline. You will see how the faculty of istinbat distinguishes a true faqih from a mere transmitter, and how the ikhtilaf on 'every mujtahid is correct' resolves without undermining the certainty of the derived ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objections to certainty of fiqh
The passage provides three answers to the objection that fiqh cannot be called 'ilm because it includes zanni qiyasi rulings. List each answer and its basis.
The first answer excludes qiyas; the second accepts zann but analogizes to other 'ulums; the third includes qiyas and explains its certainty.
2
Analyze the matn definition from Al-Talwih
Examine the definitions: 'بل هو العلم' and the restriction to 'كل الأحكام'. Understand how the plural 'ahkam' implies a minimum of three rulings for the title faqih.
The matn defines fiqh as knowledge of all rulings, but the sharh clarifies that 'all' is used to exclude partial knowledge.
3
Understand the condition of malakat al-istinbat
The passage states that mere knowledge of language and text is not enough; one must have a faculty to derive branches. Identify what this faculty entails.
Malakat al-istinbat is a trained capacity to perform qiyas and istidlal.
4
Trace the dalil for certainty of qiyas
The Quranic command 'fai'tabiru' and the hadith of Mu'adh are cited. Explain how each proves that qiyasi rulings are recognized by Shari'ah and thus certain in obligation.
The Prophet's tacit approval (taqrir) is a valid source of Sunnah.
5
Apply the taswib dispute to certainty
The passage presents two views on whether every mujtahid is correct. Show how both views still lead to the conclusion that qiyasi rulings are certain for the mujtahid.
The key distinction: certainty is about obligation to act, not about correspondence with the divine ruling in reality.
